General Knowledge
1. Kurt Waldheim was an Austrian diplomat and
statesman who served two terms as the
secretary general of the United
Nations. (Fourth)
Associated Information:
Kurt Waldheim (1918-2007) was an Austrian diplomat
and politician who:
• Served as UN Secretary-General for two terms (1972-
1977 and 1977-1981), focusing on issues like
disarmament, human rights, and sustainable
development.
• Was elected President of Austria (1986-1992), despite
controversy surrounding his wartime past.

5. SCO, Shanghai Cooperation Organization was
established in: (2001)
Associated Information:
The Shanghai Cooperation Organization (SCO),
established in 2001, is a key platform for regional
cooperation in security, economic development, and
cultural exchange. It comprises 9 member states, 3
observer states, and 14 dialogue partners. Pakistan, a
member since 2017, actively contributes to regional
stability, economic growth, and diplomatic engagement.
SCO Membership (as of 2025)
• Founding Members: China, Russia, Kazakhstan,
Kyrgyzstan, Tajikistan, Uzbekistan.
• New Members: India (2017), Pakistan (2017), Iran
(2023).
• Observer States: Afghanistan, Mongolia, Belarus (in
process of full membership since 2022).
• Dialogue Partners: Azerbaijan, Armenia, Bahrain,
Egypt, Cambodia, Qatar, Kuwait, Maldives,
Myanmar, Nepal, UAE, Saudi Arabia, Türkiye, Sri
Lanka.
SCO's Primary Goals
1. Promote trust and cooperation among members.
2. Enhance regional security and stability.
3. Facilitate economic and trade development.
4. Foster cultural and humanitarian ties.
Key Areas of Focus
• Counter-Terrorism and Security: Combat
terrorism, extremism, and separatism.
• Trade and Economic Cooperation: Enhance
connectivity and integration.
• Energy and Transportation: Collaborate on
resources and infrastructure.
• Culture and Education: Encourage cultural and
academic exchanges.
• Science and Technology: Foster innovation and
scientific collaboration.
Pakistan's Role and Interests
• Membership: Became a full member in 2017.
• Key Interests:
o Strengthening regional security through joint
efforts.
o Promoting economic growth and trade
opportunities.
o Collaborating on energy and transportation
projects to meet needs and boost connectivity.
o Expanding access to Central Asian markets and
resources.
o Enhancing cultural and humanitarian ties with
member states.
o Addressing security challenges through the
Regional Anti-Terrorist Structure (RATS).
o Diversifying foreign policy by engaging with broader
partners.
o Playing a proactive role in regional diplomacy and
multilateral forums.
Pakistan's Contributions to the SCO
• RATS Participation: Active involvement in counter-
terrorism efforts.
• Peace and Security Initiatives: Contributions to
maintaining regional stability.
• Economic Collaborations: Engagement in energy,
transportation, and trade projects.

• Cultural and Educational Exchanges: Promoting
people-to-people links to member states.
• Multilateral Forums: Active participation in SCO
summits and dialogues, reinforcing its commitment
to regional cooperation.
6. Amnesty International is an International non-
governmental organization for Human Rights it
was founded in: (1961)
Associated Information:
Amnesty International is an international NGO
committed to protecting and promoting human rights
worldwide. It was founded on May 28, 1961, by British
lawyer Peter Benenson, inspired by the imprisonment of
two Portuguese students for toasting to freedom. Its
headquarters, the International Secretariat, is based in
London, UK.
Mission
To protect people where justice, freedom, truth, and
dignity are denied.
Focus Areas
• Safeguarding against human rights violations.
• Advancing international human rights law and
standards.
• Campaigning for justice and accountability.
Key Issues
• Death Penalty
• Torture and Ill-Treatment
• Freedom of Expression
• Refugees and Migrants
• Women's Rights
• LGBTQI+ Rights
• Indigenous Peoples' Rights
• Economic, Social, and Cultural Rights
Organizational Structure
1. International Secretariat: Coordinates global
operations from London.
2. Sections: Country-specific offices managing local
advocacy and campaigns.
3. Members and Activists: Millions of global
supporters driving campaigns and activism.
Activities
• Research and Reporting: Exposing human rights
violations.
• Campaigning and Advocacy: Mobilizing public
opinion and pressuring governments.
• Lobbying and Diplomacy: Influencing policies at
national and international levels.
• Community Mobilization and Activism:
Empowering individuals to take action.
• Education and Awareness: Promoting global
human rights education.
Notable Campaigns
• "Write for Rights": Rallying global support for
individuals facing injustice.
• "Stop Torture": Advocating to end torture
worldwide.
• "Refugees Welcome": Supporting refugees' and
migrants' rights.
• "Human Rights Defenders": Backing individuals
risking their lives for human rights.
• "Climate Justice": Linking human rights with
climate advocacy.

10. World Food Program an organization of the
United Nations was created at the behest of:
(Dwight Eisenhower)
Associated Information:
The World Food Programme (WFP), the largest
humanitarian organization of the United Nations, was
established in 1961 to provide multilateral food aid
through the UN system. The idea was initially proposed
In 1959 by George McGovern, head of the U.S. Food for
Peace Program, and Senator Hubert Humphrey. It was
later championed by the administration of President
Dwight D. Eisenhower. The concept gained traction at
the 1961 Food and Agricultural Organization (FAO)
Conference, leading to a three-year experimental launch
by the FAO and the United Nations General Assembly in
1963.
Since its inception, the WFP has been dedicated to
combating hunger and ensuring food security
worldwide. Its work includes providing emergency food
assistance, improving nutrition, and fostering resilience
in vulnerable communities. Operating in over 80
countries, the WFP supports millions affected by
conflicts, disasters, and poverty, making it a cornerstone
of global humanitarian efforts.
11. Asabiyya, a concept of social solidarity with an
emphasis on unity, was given by (Ibn e
Khaldun)
Associated Information:
Asabiyya
1. Definition:
o Asabiyya is an Arabic term that refers to a sense of
community, solidarity, or group loyalty.
2. Core Elements:
o It encompasses concepts such as kinship ties,
shared identity, social cohesion, and group loyalty.
3. Social and Political Significance:
o Asabiyya is a powerful force shaping social and
political dynamics in many Arab and Muslim
societies.
o It fosters unity within groups but can also
contribute to sectarianism, tribal conflicts,
nepotism, and exclusive nationalism.
4. Historical Context:
o The concept of asabiyya was introduced by Ibn
Khaldun, a 14th-century historian and philosopher,
in his seminal work "The Muqaddimah".
5. Ibn Khaldun's Perspective:

Pakistan Studies
18. Who was the first governor general of the State
Bank of Pakistan? (Zahid Hussain)
19. When Balochistan was given the status of
province? (1970)
Associated Information:
Northern Baluchistan became a Presidency and province
of British India In 1877, administered directly by the
British.
- Four princely states:
- Makran: A coastal state with its capital in Turbat
- Kharan: A small state in the northeast, known for its
natural gas reserves
- Las Bela: A state in the southeast, with its capital in
Bela
- Kalat: The largest and most influential state, with its
capital in Kalat City
These states were recognized and reinforced in
Balochistan through treaties and agreements with the
British.
- The British exploited Balochistan's natural resources,
including coal, chromite, and natural gas
- The region saw significant infrastructure development,
including the construction of railways, roads, and
telegraph lines
Path to Provincial Status:
- After Pakistan's partition In 1947, Balochistan became
part of the new country
- Initially, Balochistan was not given provincial status and
was instead governed directly by the federal
government
- The Province of Balochistan Act was passed in 1970,
granting Balochistan provincial status on July 1, 1970
- This marked a significant milestone in Balochistan's
political evolution, recognizing its distinct identity and
granting it greater autonomy

23. Katas Raj Temples are revered Hindu temples
in Pakistan. In which district do the temples
reside? (Chakwal)
Associated Information:
Katas Raj Temples is a historic and sacred temple
complex in Pakistan.
Location: Situated in the Chakwal District of Punjab,
Pakistan.
Religious Significance: Dedicated to Lord Shiva, it is a
revered pilgrimage site for Hindus.
Historical Origins: Believed to date back to the 7th
century CE, with some legends tracing its history even
further to ancient times.
Architectural Features: The temples feature intricate
carvings and sculptures, showcasing the artistic and
cultural heritage of the region.
Pilgrimage Importance: Draws Hindu pilgrims from
around the world, particularly during the Shivaratri
festival.
Restoration Efforts: The complex has undergone
significant restoration work to preserve its historic and
religious significance.
Cultural Heritage: Recognized as an important cultural
and historical landmark In Pakistan, reflecting the
country's diverse religious and architectural history.

27. IRSA, Indus River System Authority was
established for regulating and monitoring the
distribution of water sources of Indus Rivers in:
(1992)
Explanation of IRSA's Establishment:
The Indus River System Authority (IRSA) was
established in 1991 following the Water Apportionment
Accord (WAA) of 1991, which set the foundation for the
regulation and monitoring of water distribution among
Pakistan's provinces.
While IRSA began its operations in 1991, its legal
framework was formally defined through the Indus
River System Authority Act (1992), which was enacted
to outline its roles, responsibilities, and operational
authority in managing the Indus River system's water
resources.
28. Sindh Sagar Doab is one of the five major
doabs of the Punjab province. It is the tract of
land between the following rivers (Indus and
Jhelum)

--- Page 4 ---
16
Advanced PPSC MCQs Papers (Volume 2)
Associated Information:
In Punjab, Pakistan, the term "doab" refers to the tract
of land between two rivers, derived from Persian,
where "do" means two and "ab" means water or river.
These regions are critical to Punjab's identity and
agriculture. The province's name itself means "land of
five rivers", and the doabs reflect the confluence and
separation of these rivers. Below are the main doabs of
Punjab:
1. Sindh Sagar Doab (Indus-Jhelum)
Between Rivers: Indus and Jhelum
Districts: Attock, Rawalpindi, Jhelum, Chakwal, parts of
Mianwali
Features: Largest and driest of all doabs, includes parts
of the Potohar Plateau
Notable Area: The Thal region, a desert tract, lies within
its southern zone.
2. Chaj Doab (Jhelum-Chenab)
Between Rivers: Jhelum and Chenab
Districts: Gujrat, Mandi Bahauddin, parts of Jhelum and
Sargodha
Features: Fertile land, historically part of British canal
colonization.
Rechna Doab (Chenab-Ravi)
Between Rivers: Chenab and Ravi
Districts: Faisalabad, Gujranwala, Sheikhupura, Sialkot
Features: Densely populated and agriculturally rich,
home to key cities and industries.
Bari Doab (Ravi-Beas/Sutlej)
Between Rivers: Ravi and the now-defunct Beas
(merged with Sutlej)
Districts: Lahore, Kasur, Okara, parts of Sahiwal
Features: Includes Lahore, the provincial capital;
significant historical and cultural value.
Bist Doab / Jalandhar Doab (Beas-Sutlej)
Between Rivers: Beas and Sutlej
Present Location: Mainly in Indian Punjab
Pakistani Portion: Marginal areas in Kasur and Okara
near Sutlej
Note: Not a major doab in Pakistan's geography but
relevant historically.
Indus-Sutlej Doab (Rarely Mentioned)
Between Rivers: Indus and Sutlej
Features: Arid and less developed, sparsely populated
Districts (Partially): Some areas in southern Punjab (e.g.,
parts of Bahawalpur)
These doabs not only define the physical geography of
Punjab but but also play a crucial role in its agriculture,
settlement patterns, and historical development. The
British colonial canal system was largely developed
across these doabs, shaping Punjab into the agrarian
heartland of Pakistan.

32. Which gas field among the following is the
second largest gas field in Pakistan which is
being operated by OGDCL? (Qadirpur Gas
field)
Associated Information:
Gas fields in Pakistan:
1. Sui Gas Field
o Location: Balochistan
o Operator: Pakistan Petroleum Limited (PPL)
o Significance: Largest natural gas field in Pakistan
with estimated reserves of 1.6 trillion cubic feet.
o Discovery: 1952
o Operational Since: 1955
o Ranking: 1st (largest)
2. Qadirpur Gas Field
o Location: Sindh
o Operator: Oil and Gas Development Company
Limited (OGDCL)
o Significance: Major contributor to Pakistan's
natural gas production.
o Discovery: March 1990
o Joint Venture Partners: OGDCL, KUFPEC Pakistan,
PKP Exploration Ltd, PKP2 Exploration Ltd, and
Petroleum Pakistan Limited.
o Ranking: 2nd (second-largest)
3. Uch Gas Field
o Location: Balochistan
o Operator: Pakistan Petroleum Limited (PPL)
o Significance: Holds reserves in Habib Rahi
Limestone, Sui Main, and Upper Limestone
reservoirs.
o Discovery: Shortly after the discovery of Sui Gas
Field.
o Production Began: 1987
o Ranking: 3rd (third-largest)
4. Kandhkot Gas Field
o Location: Sindh
o Operator: Oil and Gas Development Company
Limited (OGDCL)
o Significance: Contains reserves in Habib Rahi
Limestone, Sui Main, and Upper Limestone
reservoirs.
o Discovery: After Sui Gas Field
o Production Began: 1987
o Ranking: 4th (fourth-largest)
5. Sakessar Gas Field
o Location: Punjab
o Operator: Pakistan Petroleum Limited (PPL)
o Significance: Contributes to Pakistan's overall gas
supply.
o Ranking: 5th (smallest)
Note: The rankings are based on the significance and size
of reserves, with the Sui Gas Field being the largest and
the Sakessar Gas Field the smallest among the listed
fields.

Current Affairs
38. Pakistan's population is what per cent of the
world's population? (2.65%)
Explanation:
1. Population in 2019:
o Pakistan's population was approximately 216.6
million in 2019, according to reliable demographic data.
o The global population in 2019 was about 7.7
billion.
o Based on these figures, Pakistan's share of the
global population in 2019 was 2.65%.
2. Current Population as of January 2025:
o Pakistan's population is estimated to be around
255 million as of January 2025.
o The global population is projected to be
approximately 8.2 billion in 2025.
o Using these figures, Pakistan's percentage of the
global population is approximately 3.1%.
Key Points:
• 2019 Percentage: 2.65% of the global population.
• 2025 Percentage: Approximately 3.1% of the
global population.
This increase highlights Pakistan's significant population
growth rate, making it the 5th most populous country
globally.

Geography
42. Kirthar Range is a mountain range in Pakistan
extending about 190 miles. In which province of
Pakistan does the range's largest portion
reside? (Sindh)
Associated Information:
The Kirthar Range is a mountain range located in the
southwestern part of Sindh, Pakistan. It is a subset of the
Kirthar Mountains, which stretch for approximately 300
kilometers (186 miles) from the Bolan Pass in
Balochistan to the Arabian Sea.
Key Facts about the Kirthar Range:
Location: Sindh, Pakistan
Length: Approximately 300 km (186 miles)
Width: 40-80 km (25-50 miles)
Highest Point: Gorakh Hill, 2,121 meters (6,959 ft)
Geology: Primarily composed of limestone, sandstone,
and gypsum
Climate: Arid to semi-arid
Vegetation: Scrub forest, grasslands, and succulent
plants
Wildlife: Home to species such as the Sindh ibex, urial,
and Houbara bustard.
Geographical Spread: The Kirthar Range primarily lies in
Sindh Province, extending from the southern region of
Sindh into the northeastern part of Balochistan. The
largest portion of the range is in Sindh, and it serves as a
natural boundary between the two provinces.
43. What is the length of Karakoram Highway? (810
miles)
Associated Information:
Comprehensive Associate Information
The Karakoram Highway (KKH), often referred to as the
"Eighth Wonder of the World," is a remarkable feat of
engineering that connects Pakistan and China. It is the
highest paved international road globally and plays a
significant role in economic, cultural, and strategic
contexts.
Length and Route
• Total Length: The KKH spans approximately 1,300
kilometers (810 miles) in total.
o Pakistani Section: About 887 kilometers (551
miles) from Hasan Abdal in Punjab to the
Khunjerab Pass in Gilgit-Baltistan.
o Chinese Section: Approximately 413 kilometers
(257 miles) from the Khunjerab Pass to Kashgar in
the Xinjiang Uyghur Autonomous Region.
Key Features and Historical Significance
1. Construction and Origins:
o The KKH was officially completed in 1978, following
two decades of construction that involved over
15,000 workers from Pakistan and China.
o Built on the foundation of the ancient Silk Road, it
represents the strong partnership between the two
nations.
2. High-Altitude Passes:
o Muztagh Pass (also called Mustagh Pass) lies on the
international border between Pakistan (Gilgit-Baltistan)
and China (Xinjiang), with an elevation of approximately

Advanced PPSC MCQs Papers (Volume 2)
- Blood Glucose Test: Measures blood sugar levels and helps diagnose diabetes.
- Lipid Panel: Measures cholesterol and triglyceride levels, assessing heart disease risk.
- Liver Function Tests (LFTs): Evaluate liver health and detect liver damage or disease.
- Kidney Function Tests (KFTs): Assess kidney function and detect kidney disease.
- Thyroid Function Tests (TFTs): Measure thyroid hormone levels and diagnose thyroid disorders.
- Electrolyte Panel: Measures electrolyte levels (sodium, potassium, chloride) and assesses fluid balance.
- C-Reactive Protein (CRP) Test: Indicates inflammation in the body and assesses the risk of heart disease.
- Hemoglobin A1c (HbA1c) Test: Measures average blood sugar levels over the past 2-3 months and monitors diabetes control.

**Imaging Tests:**
- X-ray: Uses radiation to create images of bones and internal organs, detecting fractures, infections, and tumors.
- CT Scan (Computed Tomography): Combines X-ray images from different angles to create detailed cross-sectional images of organs, bones, and soft tissues.
- MRI (Magnetic Resonance Imaging): Uses magnetic fields and radio waves to create detailed images of organs, soft tissues, bone, and virtually all other internal body structures.
- Ultrasound: Uses high-frequency sound waves to create real-time images of internal organs, blood vessels, and soft tissues, often used for pregnancy and abdominal issues.

**Urinalysis:**
- Urinalysis: Analyzes urine samples to detect urinary tract infections, kidney disease, and other metabolic disorders.

**Other Specialized Tests:**
- Electrocardiogram (ECG/EKG): Records electrical activity of the heart, detecting heart rhythm abnormalities and heart attacks.
- Endoscopy: Uses a flexible tube with a camera to visualize the digestive tract, diagnosing conditions like ulcers, inflammation, and tumors.
- Biopsy: Involves removing a small tissue sample for microscopic examination, diagnosing cancer and other diseases.
- Genetic Testing: Analyzes DNA to identify genetic mutations, diagnosing inherited diseases and assessing cancer risk.
- Allergy Testing: Identifies specific allergens that trigger allergic reactions, helping to manage allergies.
- Bone Density Scan (DEXA Scan): Measures bone mineral density, diagnosing osteoporosis and assessing fracture risk.
- Mammogram: X-ray of the breast to detect breast cancer.
- Pap Smear: Screens for cervical cancer by collecting cells from the cervix.
- Colonoscopy: Examines the colon for polyps or cancer.
- Stress Test: Evaluates heart function during exercise.
- Spirometry: Measures lung function to diagnose respiratory conditions.
- Sleep Study (Polysomnography): Monitors sleep patterns to diagnose sleep disorders.
- Nerve Conduction Study (NCS): Measures nerve function to diagnose nerve damage.
- Electroencephalogram (EEG): Records brain activity to diagnose neurological disorders.
- Lumbar Puncture (Spinal Tap): Collects cerebrospinal fluid for analysis to diagnose neurological conditions.
- Arteriogram/Angiogram: Visualizes blood vessels to detect blockages or abnormalities.
- Echocardiogram: Ultrasound of the heart to assess its structure and function.
- Holter Monitor: Portable ECG device that records heart activity over 24-48 hours.
- Pulmonary Function Tests (PFTs): Comprehensive assessment of lung function.
- Blood Culture: Detects bacteria or other microorganisms in the blood.
- Urine Culture: Detects bacteria in the urine to diagnose UTIs.
- Stool Culture: Detects bacteria or parasites in stool samples.
- Throat Swab: Collects samples from the throat to diagnose infections.
- Wound Culture: Identifies bacteria in a wound to guide treatment.
- Cerebrospinal Fluid (CSF) Analysis: Examines CSF to diagnose neurological infections or disorders.
- Joint Fluid Analysis: Examines fluid from a joint to diagnose arthritis or infection.
- Bone Marrow Biopsy: Collects bone marrow for examination to diagnose blood disorders or cancer.
- Lymph Node Biopsy: Removes a lymph node for examination to diagnose infections or cancer.
- Skin Biopsy: Removes a skin sample for examination to diagnose skin conditions or cancer.
- Muscle Biopsy: Collects muscle tissue for examination to diagnose muscle disorders.
- Liver Biopsy: Removes liver tissue for examination to diagnose liver diseases.
- Kidney Biopsy: Collects kidney tissue for examination to diagnose kidney diseases.
- Lung Biopsy: Removes lung tissue for examination to diagnose lung conditions.
- Brain Biopsy: Collects brain tissue for examination to diagnose brain tumors or disorders.
- Endoscopic Ultrasound (EUS): Combines endoscopy with ultrasound to visualize the digestive tract and surrounding organs.
- Capsule Endoscopy: Uses a small camera capsule to capture images of the small intestine.
- ERCP (Endoscopic Retrograde Cholangiopancreatography): Visualizes bile and pancreatic ducts to diagnose conditions like gallstones or tumors.
- PET Scan (Positron Emission Tomography): Uses a radioactive tracer to detect metabolic activity in cells, often used for cancer detection.
- SPECT Scan (Single-Photon Emission Computed Tomography): Uses a radioactive tracer to create 3D images of organs and blood flow.
- Nuclear Medicine Scan: Uses radioactive substances to diagnose and treat various diseases.
- Angiography: X-ray imaging of blood vessels using a contrast agent.
- Venography: X-ray imaging of veins using a contrast agent.
- Myelography: X-ray imaging of the spinal cord and nerves using a contrast agent.
- Discography: Injects contrast into spinal discs to identify sources of back pain.
- Arthrogram: Injects contrast into a joint to visualize its structures.
- Hysterosalpingography (HSG): X-ray of the uterus and fallopian tubes to assess fertility.
- Intravenous Pyelogram (IVP): X-ray of the urinary tract using a contrast agent.
- Barium Swallow/Enema: X-ray of the digestive tract using barium contrast.
- Urodynamic Testing: Evaluates bladder and urethra function.
- Cystoscopy: Visualizes the bladder and urethra using a thin scope.
- Colposcopy: Examines the cervix, vagina, and vulva for abnormal cells.
- Laparoscopy: Minimally invasive surgery using a small incision and a camera.
- Thoracoscopy: Minimally invasive surgery of the chest cavity.
- Mediastinoscopy: Examines the mediastinum (space between the lungs) for abnormalities.
- Bronchoscopy: Examines the airways and lungs using a thin scope.
- Laryngoscopy: Examines the larynx (voice box) and vocal cords.
- Otoscopy: Examines the ear canal and eardrum.
- Ophthalmoscopy: Examines the back of the eye (fundus).
- Slit Lamp Exam: Magnified examination of the eye's front structures.
- Tonometry: Measures intraocular pressure to screen for glaucoma.
- Visual Field Test: Measures peripheral vision to detect vision loss.
- Fundus Photography: Captures images of the retina and optic nerve.
- Optical Coherence Tomography (OCT): High-resolution imaging of the retina.
- Fluorescein Angiography: Uses a dye to visualize blood vessels in the retina.
- Electroretinography (ERG): Measures electrical activity of the retina.
- Visual Evoked Potentials (VEP): Measures brain's response to visual stimuli.
- Auditory Brainstem Response (ABR): Measures brain's response to sound.
- Otoacoustic Emissions (OAEs): Measures sounds produced by the inner ear.
- Tympanometry: Measures middle ear function.
- Audiometry: Measures hearing sensitivity.
- Speech Audiometry: Evaluates speech understanding.
- Bone Conduction Audiometry: Measures hearing through bone vibrations.
- Impedance Audiometry: Assesses middle ear function.
- Electronystagmography (ENG): Records eye movements to assess balance.
- Videonystagmography (VNG): Video recording of eye movements for balance assessment.
- Caloric Testing: Stimulates the inner ear with temperature changes to assess balance.
- Posturography: Measures balance and stability.
- Vestibular Evoked Myogenic Potentials (VEMPs): Measures muscle responses to sound to assess inner ear function.
- Rotary Chair Test: Assesses balance system by rotating the patient.
- Fistula Test: Detects abnormal connection between inner ear and middle ear.
- Glycerol Test: Evaluates inner ear fluid balance in Meniere's disease.
- Electrocochleography (ECochG): Measures electrical activity of the inner ear.

**Conclusion:**
Medical tests are crucial for accurate diagnosis, effective treatment, and ongoing monitoring of health conditions. By understanding the purpose and procedure of various tests, healthcare professionals can make informed decisions and provide personalized care to patients. Regular screenings and timely diagnostic tests play a vital role in maintaining overall health and well-being.

Associated Information:
Villi are small, finger-like structures that line the walls of
the small intestine. They are responsible for increasing
the surface area of the intestine, allowing for more
efficient absorption of nutrients from food into the
bloodstream.

85. At which temperature do bacteria die? (120 °F)
Associated Information:
Scientific research confirms that most non-spore-
forming bacteria are killed at temperatures above
149°F (65°C). For example, food safety standards by the

--- Page 3 ---
Papers of 2019
25
U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA) and Centers
for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) state that:
167°F (75°C) kills most common foodborne bacteria
within minutes.
•
212°F (100°C) (boiling point of water) destroys most
bacteria rapidly.
Spore-forming bacteria, such as Clostridium
botulinum, can survive even boiling water and require
pressure cooking at 250°F (121°C) to ensure complete
ellmination (as per USDA guidelines for canning).
The commonly cited "Danger Zone" for bacterial growth
is 40°F to 140°F (4°C to 60°C). Temperatures below 40°F
slow bacterial growth, while above 140°F, bacteria begin
to die.
Among the given options, 120°F (49°C) is the closest, but
still too low to effectively kill most pathogens.
Nonetheless, since none of the options reflect the
scientifically accurate killing temperature (2149°F),
option (D) 120°F is the most approximate based on
proximity.
Conclusion: 120°F is not sufficient for killing most
bacteria, but is the nearest correct choice among
incorrect options.

22. By which amendment Article 58-(2)(b) removed
from constitution? (18th)
Association Information:
Article 58(2)(b) and Its Evolution
Article 58(2)(b), a provision in Pakistan's Constitution,
allowed the President to dissolve the National Assembly
at their discretion. It underwent significant changes
through various amendments, reflecting shifts in the
Introduced by General Zia-ul-Haq to grant the
President unilateral power to dissolve the National
Assembly in cases where governance could not
function as per the Constitution.
Shifted Pakistan toward
system.
13th Amendment (1997):
a
semi-presidential
Context: Removed the President's unilateral power
to dissolve the National Assembly.
Result: The President could no longer invoke Article
58(2)(b) without the Prime Minister's advice,
restoring parliamentary sovereignty.
17th Amendment (2003):
General Pervez Musharraf restored Article 58(2)(b)
to allow the President to dissolve the National
Assembly.
Justified as a check and balance on the Prime
Minister's authority, but it weakened parliamentary
autonomy.
18th Amendment (2010):
Under Asif Ali Zardari's government, Article
58(2)(b) was permanently removed.
Strengthened parliamentary democracy by
ensuring the President could no longer dissolve the
National Assembly unilaterally.
Key Impacts
•
•
•
•
8th Amendment (1985): Established presidential
dissolution powers.
13th Amendment (1997): Removed the President's
unilateral power to dissolve the National Assembly,
making dissolution subject to the Prime Minister's
advice.
17th Amendment (2003): Restored the President's
powers to dissolve the assembly.
18th Amendment (2010): Fully abolished Article
58(2)(b), reinforcing a parliamentary system.
The history of Article 58(2)(b) showcases a struggle for
balance between presidential authority and
parliamentary democracy in Pakistan. The 13th
Amendment marked an important step toward curbing
presidential powers, while the 18th Amendment
ultimately ensured a stable and sovereign parliamentary
system by permanently removing this provision.

25. In which field Dr Abdul Salam won Nobel Prize?
(Physics)
Associated Information:
Dr. Abdul Salam (1926-1996) was a Pakistani theoretical
physicist and mathematician who made significant
contributions to the field of physics. He was a leading
figure in the development of the Standard Model of
particle physics and was awarded the Nobel Prize in
Physics in 1979 for his work on the electroweak
unification of the fundamental forces.
Dr. Salam was born in Jhang, Pakistan and studied at the
University of Punjab and the University of Cambridge. He
later moved to the Imperial College London, where he
spent most of his career.
Dr. Salam's research focused on the unified field
theories, and he was one of the first scientists to
recognize the importance of symmetry principles in
particle physics. He also made significant contributions
to the development of quantum field theory and the
theory of superconductivity.

Geography
35. Antarctica is located in: (South pole)
Associated Information:
Antarctica is the frozen continent at the bottom of the
world. Here are some Interesting facts about Antarctica:
1. Coldest place on Earth: Antarctica is the coldest
continent, with temperatures reaching as low as -93.2°C
(-135.8°F) in winter.
1
2. Largest single mass of Ice: Antarctica covers about 98%
of its land area with ice, which is up to 4,776 meters
(15,667 feet) thick in some areas!
3. Unique wildlife: Antarctica is home to penguins, seals,
and whales, which have adapted to the extreme
conditions.
4. No permanent residents: Antarctica has no permanent
human residents, only temporary scientists and
researchers at various stations.
5. Protected by treaty: The Antarctic Treaty System
regulates international relations and environmental
protection for the continent.
36. The highest Waterfall is: (Angel)
Associated Information:
Denmark Stralt Cataract (Underwater Waterfall): The
Denmark Strait cataract, hidden beneath the Atlantic
Ocean between Greenland and Iceland, holds the title of
the world's largest waterfall. It plunges an astonishing
11,500 feet (3,505 meters) downward, carrying around
175 million cubic feet (5 million cubic meters) of water
per second. This underwater cascade significantly
surpasses any land-based waterfall. Even Niagara Falls
seems modest compared to this aquatic marvel.
But what causes this underwater spectacle? It's all about
temperature and density. The ultra-cold Arctic waters
from the Greenland Sea meet the slightly warmer waters
of the Irminger Sea. Since cold water is denser, it sinks
down through the warmer water, creating this colossal
cataract.
Angel Falls (Land-Based Waterfall): Angel Falls, also
known as Salto Angel, resides in Venezuela. It's the
world's tallest uninterrupted waterfall, with a staggering
height of 979 meters (3,212 feet) and a single plunge of
807 meters (2,648 feet). Imagine standing at the top of a
table-top mountain (tepul) called Auyan-tepui, and
watching the water plummet dramatically into the
Canaima National Park. Angel Falls is named after Jimmie
Angel, the first aviator to fly over it. His ashes were even
scattered in the falls in 1960.
Victoria Falls (The Largest by Width): Victoria Falls,
located on the border of Zimbabwe and Zambia, boasts
an impressive width of approximately 1,700 meters (1.7
kilometers). It create a mist visible from miles away and
known locally as "The Smoke That Thunders". That's one
and a half times wider than Niagara Falls! Its horseshoe
shape adds to its grandeur. In terms of sheer volume,
Victoria Falls doesn't disappoint either. During peak
floodwaters (around mid-April), roughly 625 million
liters of water flow over the edge every minute. While
Angel holds the height record, Victoria Falls wins
when it comes to overall dimensions and flow rate. It's
:
a dynamic curtain of water that changes dramatically
throughout the year, depending on the Zambezi River's
cycle of rising and falling.

42. Which is the shortest of the Solar System?
(Mercury)
Associated Information:
Mercury is the smallest planet in our Solar System, with
a diameter of approximately 4,879 kilometers (3,031
miles). It is also the closest planet to the Sun and has the
shortest orbital period, completing one rotation around
the Sun in about 88 Earth days. In fact, Mercury is the
smallest of all the planets in our Solar System, followed
by Mars, Venus, Earth, Neptune, Uranus, Saturn, and
Jupiter, in order of increasing size.
Here is a list of the planets in our Solar System, in order
from smallest to largest, along with their diameters:
1. Mercury - 4,879 km (3,031 miles)
2. Mars - 6,794 km (4,220 miles)
3. Venus - 12,104 km (7,521 miles)
4. Earth - 12,742 km (7,918 miles)
5. Neptune - 49,528 km (30,777 miles)
6. Uranus - 51,118 km (31,763 miles)
7. Saturn - 116,464 km (72,337 miles)
8. Jupiter - 142,984 km (88,846 miles)
43. Which planet it the farthest from the sun?
(Neptune)
44. Which planet is visible from Earth? (Venus)
45. Panama Canal links: (North and South
America)
Associated Information:
The Panama Canal is an 82 km (51 miles) man-made
waterway in Panama, connecting the Atlantic and
Pacific Oceans. Opened on August 15, 1914, it
significantly reduced travel time for maritime trade,
avoiding the long journey around Cape Horn.
Key Points:

51. Which continent is double in size than
Australia? (Antarctica)
Associated Information:
The 7 continents listed in order from largest to smallest
based on their land area are:
1. Asia: 44,579,000 km² / 17,212,000 sq mi
2. Africa: 30,370,000 km² / 11,700,000 sq mi
3. North America: 21,495,000 km² / 8,300,000 sq mi
4. South America: 17,819,000 km² / 6,879,000 sq mi
5. Antarctica: 14,000,000 km² / 5,400,000 sq mi
6. Europe: 10,180,000 km² / 3,930,000 sq mi
7. Australia: 7,617,930 km² / 2,936,557 sq mi
Clarification:
This is a tricky question, and many students mistakenly
choose Africa because it is significantly larger than
Australia. However, the question specifically asks for a
continent that is double the size of Australia, not more.
• Africa: While it is approximately 4 times the size of
Australia (30,370,000 km² vs. 7,617,930 km²), it far
exceeds the "double" condition.
• Antarctica: With an area of 14,000,000 km²,.
Antarctica is approximately 1.84 times the size of
Australia. This makes it the closest match to the
"double" condition described in the question.
Calculation to Confirm Antarctica's Fit:
14,000,000 km² (Antarctica)÷7,617,930 km² (Australia)≈
1.84
Thus, Antarctica is nearly double the size of Australia.
Key Insight for Students:
• Africa is significantly larger than Australia but does
not meet the "double" condition.
• Antarctica is the correct answer because its size is
closest to twice that of Australia.
52. Which continent has no desert? (Europe)
Associated Information:
Europe is the only continent without a desert. Its unique
geography, proximity to oceans, majestic mountain
ranges (like the Alps and Pyrenees), and prevailing wind
patterns have kept deserts at bay. While other
continents boast famous deserts like the Sahara, Gobi,
and Great Victoria, Europe remains desert-free.
Antarctica, on the other hand, is a frozen continent with
extreme cold temperatures and vast ice sheets, making
it the ultimate anti-desert.

55. The longest mountain range is: (Andes)
56. In which region New Zealand is situated?
(Oceania)
Explanation: New Zealand is located in the Oceania
region, specifically within the Australasian sub-region.
Although often grouped with Australia politically and
culturally, New Zealand is not part of the Australian
continent. Geologically, New Zealand sits on its own
separate tectonic plate, forming a nearly submerged
continent called Zealandia. About 94% of Zealandia lies
under the ocean, leaving only New Zealand and nearby
islands above sea level. Scientists recognize Zealandia as
a distinct continent, but since it is mostly underwater, it
is not shown in standard atlases. Therefore, New
Zealand is considered part of Oceania, while resting on
the Zealandia continental mass.
57. Which is the known as the 8th continent?
(Eastern Europe, Oceania, Central America:
None of these)
Associated Information:
Oceania is actually not a continent in the strict
geographical sense; rather, it is a region in the Pacific
Ocean that includes Australia, New Zealand, Papua New
Guinea, and numerous island groups. It is sometimes
referred to as the "8th continent" due to its unique
cultural and geographic identity, but officially, the 7-
continent model is recognized. The term "Oceania" was
first used by French geographer Dumont d'Urville in
1831, and has since gained wide acceptance as a
regional classification, not as a separate continent.

60. Which is the largest river among Ravi, Chenab
and Sutlej? (Chenab)
Explanation:
The Chenab River is the largest among the three rivers
in water flow and significance within Pakistan. It has a
total length of 974 km (605 miles), with 730.6 km
flowing through Pakistan. Its water flow of 977.3 cubic
meters per second is the highest among the three,
making it an essential source of water for irrigation,
hydroelectric power, and agriculture. Originating in
Himachal Pradesh, India, It enters Pakistan and serves as
a primary water resource under the Indus Waters
Treaty. Due to its consistent water supply and vast basin
area, it plays a crucial role in Pakistan's river system.
The Sutlej River is the longest in total length, spanning
1,450 km (900 miles), but only 530.6 km of it lies within
Pakistan. Much of its water is diverted in India for
irrigation and hydroelectric projects, leading to a
significant reduction in its flow within Pakistan. While it
is the longest overall, its contribution to Pakistan's river
system is minimal due to water diversions.
The Ravi River is the smallest among the three, with a
total length of 725 km (450 miles), of which 680.6 km
lies within Pakistan. It has the lowest water flow, as
most of its water is withheld in India, significantly
reducing its availability for irrigation and consumption.
While Sutlej is the longest in total length, Chenab has
the longest stretch within Pakistan and carries the
highest volume of water. It is also the most important
river for Pakistan's irrigation system.
Final Answer: (B) Chenab - the largest based on water
volume, longest stretch within Pakistan, and overall
importance in Pakistan's hydrological system.

The Pacific Ocean is the deepest ocean, with a maximum

Oceans in order of their maximum depth are:
1. Pacific Ocean: 36,000 feet (10,973 meters)
2. Atlantic Ocean: 28,000 feet (8,534 meters)
3. Indian Ocean: 25,000 feet (7,620 meters)
4. Arctic Ocean: 18,000 feet (5,489 meters)

63. Drugs are excreted through: (Kidney)
Associated Information:
Drugs are primarily excreted through the kidneys, which
filter waste and toxins from the blood and excrete them
in the urine. The kidneys play a crucial role in eliminating
drugs and their metabolites from the body.
Here's an overview of the other options:
(A) Liver: The liver metabolizes drugs, breaking them
down into smaller molecules, but it does not primarily
excrete them.
- (C) Stomach: The stomach is responsible for digesting
food and absorbing nutrients, not excreting drugs.
Note that some drugs may also be excreted through
other routes, such as the bile (via the liver), sweat, or
breath, but the kidneys are the primary organ
responsible for drug excretion.

72. Boiling of eggs is a: (both A and B) Physical &
Chemical change
Associated Information:
Boiling an egg is indeed both a physical and chemical
change.
Physical Change:
- The egg's physical properties change, such as its texture
and state (from liquid to solid).
- The egg white (albumen) starts as a clear, viscous liquid
and undergoes physical changes as it heats up.
- The proteins in the egg white denature, creating a
network that turns the liquid egg white into a solid.
- The egg yolk also thickens due to the heat, transitioning
from a runny liquid to a more gel-like consistency.
Chemical Change:
- The proteins in the egg white and yolk don't just
rearrange themselves; they form new bonds.
- During boiling, the proteins break their original bonds
and then form fresh bonds with neighboring proteins.
- Water molecules that were initially surrounding each
protein are pushed out, resulting in a permanent change.
- The egg white and yolk solidify, and these new
structures cannot easily revert to their original state.

74. Who presented the "Theory of Evolution"?
(Charles Darwin)
Associated Information:
The Theory of Evolution, introduced by Charles Darwin
in 1859, transformed our understanding of how species
change over time. This fundamental principle posits that
all living organisms share a common ancestor and have
evolved through natural selection, genetic drift,
mutation, and gene flow, leading to the immense
biodiversity observed today.
Although Charles Darwin and Alfred Russel Wallace
independently developed the concept of evolution by
natural selection in the mid-19th century, Darwin's
detailed exposition in On the Origin of Species (1859)
cemented the theory's scientific foundation.
Additionally, the phrase "Survival of the Fittest" was
coined by Herbert Spencer in his book Principles of

--- Page 31 ---
Biology (1864) after reading Darwin's work. Spencer
linked Darwin's biological theory to his economic and
social theories, stating:
"This survival of the fittest... is that which Mr. Darwin has
called 'natural selection', or the preservation of favoured
races in the struggle for life."

4x - 2x = 2000
2x = 2000 ⇒ x = 1000
Therefore, B has 2x = 2000
⇒ B gets 2000

96. How many masarif of Zakat mentioned in the
Holy Quran? (8)
Associated Information:
The Eight Categories of Zakat Recipients
In Surah At-Tawbah (9:60), the Holy Quran specifies
eight categories of individuals eligible to receive Zakat:
1. The Poor (Faqir) - Those who lack basic necessities
and struggle to meet their essential needs.
2. The Needy (Miskin) - Individuals facing financial
difficulties but not as destitute as the Faqir.
3. Zakat Administrators (Amil) - Officials responsible
for the collection and distribution of Zakat.
4. Those Whose Hearts Need Reconciliation
(Mu'allaf) - New converts to Islam or those whose
hearts need to be softened toward the faith.

--- Page 32 ---
32 Advanced PPSC MCQs Papers (Volume 2)
5. Liberation of Slaves (Riqab) - Enslaved Individuals
seeking freedom, with Zakat used to secure their
release or improve their conditions.
6. Debtors (Gharimin) - Those burdened by debt, with
Zakat aiding them in repayment to regain financial
stability.
7. In the Cause of Allah (Fi Sabilillah) - Funds
allocated for various charitable and religious
activities, such as supporting scholars, building
mosques, and funding educational Initiatives.
8. Stranded Travelers (Ibnu Sabil) - Wayfarers in need
of assistance with food, shelter, or transportation
during their journey.
These categories ensure that Zakat, a fundamental pillar
of Islam, serves as a means of social welfare, uplifting the
underprivileged and strengthening the Muslim
community.

15. The Chairman of Boundary Commission of the
Punjab & Bengal, was
(None of
these)
Associated Information:
Radcliffe Commission & Related Commissions
The Radcliffe Commission was established on June 30,
1947, led by Sir Cyril Radcliffe, a British lawyer and law
lord. The commission was tasked with demarcating
borders between India and Pakistan.
Related Commissions
The Boundary Commission, established in July 1947,
aimed to recommend how to divide Punjab and Bengal
between India and Pakistan. However, with little
agreement between the two sides, Sir Cyril Radcliffe
made the final determination on the frontiers. The
Boundary Commission for the provinces of Punjab and
Bengal was established in June 1947, but later split into
two separate commissions: the Bengal Boundary
Commission and the Punjab Boundary Commission, both
chaired by Sir Cyril Radcliffe. Both chaired by Sir Cyril
Radcliffe. Each commission had five members, including
a chairman and four representatives, two from the
Indian National Congress and two from the Muslim
League.
Commission's Work
The Radcliffe Commission began working on border
demarcation in July 1947 and published its report on
August 17, 1947. The report created the Radcliffe Line,
dividing British India into three main regions: India, West
Pakistan (now Pakistan), and East Pakistan (now
Bangladesh).
Criticism and Legacy
The commission has been criticized for its hasty and
arbitrary approach, ignoring cultural, linguistic, and
religious differences. The irregular borders created led
to the displacement of millions of people and ongoing
disputes and tensions between India and Pakistan over
territorial claims and border management.
Key Points
- Radcliffe Commission established on June 30, 1947
- Report published on August 17, 1947
- Created Radcliffe Line, dividing British India into three
main regions
- Criticized for hasty and arbitrary approach
- Led to displacement of millions of people
- Ongoing disputes and tensions between India and
Pakistan over territorial claims and border management
- Highlights importance of careful border demarcation
and inclusive approaches to political geography

64. Which one number will complete the series: 8,
13, 10, 15, 12, 17, 14,
Solution:
(19)
The series alternates between two sequences: one for
the odd positions and one for the even positions.
The odd positions (1st, 3rd, 5th, 7th) are: 8, 10, 12,
14. This increases by 2 each time.
•
The even positions (2nd, 4th, 6th) are: 13, 15, 17.
This also increases by 2 each time.
The next term is at the 8th position (even), so it should
be 17 + 2 = 19.
Thus, the completed series is: 8, 13, 10, 15, 12, 17,
14, 19.
65. If Mary traveled 116 miles on day 1, 130 miles
on day 2, 114 miles on day 3. How many miles
per day did she average? (120)
Solution:
Step 1: Calculate the total miles traveled
Total miles = 116 + 130 + 114 = 360 miles.
Step 2: Calculate the average miles per day
Average miles per day = Total miles / Number of
days = 360/3 = 120.

75. 15 men were hired to plough a piece of land in
10 days. How many more men will be required
to do the same job is 3 days? (35)
Solution:
To solve this problem, we need to calculate the total
work required to plough the land and then determine
how many men are needed to complete the work in 3
days.
Initially, 15 men are hired to complete the work in 10
days. Let's calculate the total work:
Total work = Number of men x Number of days
= 15 men x 10 days
= 150 man-days
To complete the same work in 3 days, we need to
calculate the number of men required:
Number of men required = Total work ÷ Number of days
= 150 man-days ÷ 3 days
= 50 men
Since we already have 15 men, we need to find out how
many more men are required:
Additional men required = Total men required - Existing
men
= 50 men - 15 men
= 35 men
The correct answer is: (A) 35

80. A is twice as good as workman as B and

81. If you pay $22.90 for a DVD that includes a 7%
sales tax, what is the price of the DVD before
the sales tax? ($21.40)
Let the original price of the DVD be x.
The sales tax is 7% of the original price, which is 0.07x.
The total price paid is the original price plus the sales tax:
Total price = Original price + Sales tax
$22.90 = x + 0.07x
Combine like terms:
$22.90 = 1.07x
Divide both sides by 1.07:
x = $22.90 / 1.07
x = $21.40
The correct answer is:
(B) $21.40

Al-Azhar University is a renowned Islamic university

culture.

universities in the world

- Known for its traditional Islamic Studies programs
- Has played a significant role in preserving and
promoting Islamic knowledge and culture
- Influential in the Islamic world and beyond

Associated Information:
ASEAN (Association of Southeast Asian Nations) is a
regional organization that comprises 10 countries in
Southeast Asia:
1. Brunei, 2. Cambodia, 3. Indonesia, 4. Laos, 5. Malaysia,
6. Myanmar (Burma), 7. Philippines, 8. Singapore, 9.
Thailand, 10. Vietnam
ASEAN aims to promote economic growth, social
progress, and cultural development in the region, while
also fostering regional peace and stability. It was
established on August 8, 1967, with the signing of the
ASEAN Declaration by the five original member
countries: Indonesia, Malaysia, Philippines, Singapore,
and Thailand. The headquarters of ASEAN is located in
Jakarta, Indonesia.
Some of ASEAN's key objectives include:
- Promoting economic growth and cooperation
- Enhancing regional peace and security
- Strengthening social and cultural bonds
- Encouraging collaboration in areas like education,
healthcare, and the environment
11. "Bootlegging" is a form of: (Smuggling)
Associated Information:
Bootlegging refers to the illegal production, distribution,
and sale of goods, often to avoid taxes, regulations, or
laws. It can involve counterfeiting, smuggling, tax
evasion, and regulatory avoidance, and can include a
range of products such as alcohol, tobacco, firearms,
narcotics, counterfeit goods, and pirated content.
Bootlegging has legal consequences and can harm
legitimate businesses, governments, and consumers.

Associated Information:
Understanding the United Nations: Organs, Programs,
Funds, and Specialized Agencies
Organs: Principal components of the UN, established by
the Charter, responsible for decision-making and policy-
setting. (e.g. General Assembly, Security Council).
Programs: Implement specific activities and projects,
usually established by the General Assembly or ECOSOC.
(e.g. UNDP, UNEP, UNICEF)
Funds: Provide financial support for specific activities or
projects, usually established by the General Assembly or
ECOSOC. (e.g. UNFPA, UNCDF, UNIFEM)
Specialized Agencies: Autonomous organizations
working within the UN system, established by
intergovernmental agreements or treaties, focusing on
specific areas. (e.g. WHO, ILO, UNESCO, ITU)

18. Which of the following political parties did not
boycott the Simon Commission? (Unionist
Party)
Explanation:
The Simon Commission (1927) was boycotted by most
major Indian political parties because no Indian was
included in it. However:
The Indian National Congress, Hindu Mahasabha,
and the mainstream All India Muslim League (under
Jinnah) boycotted the Commission.
•
The Unionist Party, which was pro-British and
powerful in Punjab, did not boycott the Commission.
•
Importantly, a breakaway faction of the Muslim
League, led by Sir Muhammad Shafi (also known as the
Lahore Group), cooperated with the Simon Commission
and even joined it, which created a split in Muslim
politics.
So, while the mainstream AIML boycotted, Shafi's
faction supported making the Unionist Party the
most accurate answer from the given options, but with
historical clarification regarding the split in AIML.

25. First President of Pakistan, Iskandar Mirza,

Associated Information:
Iskander Mirza: A Comprehensive Overview

--- Page 27 ---
Papers of 2019
39
Iskander Mirza (also spelled as Iskandar Mirza, Iskander
Ali Mirza, or Sikandar Mirza) (1899-1969) was a Pakistani
politician, statesman, and military general who played a
significant role in the country's early years. Born on
November 13, 1899 in Murshidabad, Bengal, India,
Mirza came from an elite and wealthy aristocratic family
with significant business interests.
Military Career
•
•
Mirza Joined the British Indian Army in 1920 and
served in various positions, Including as a Major
General during World War II.
After the partition in 1947, he joined the Pakistan
Army as a Major General and became a key figure in
the country's military and political circles.
Notably, Mirza served as both a Major General in the
Army and Defence Secretary in 1947, highlighting his
unique role in Pakistan's early years.
Political Career
•
•
Governor-General: Iskandar Mirza held the shortest
tenure as a governor general in Pakistan, serving
from October 6, 1955, to March 23, 1956. He was
appointed as the Governor-General in 1955 and later
became the president when Pakistan's first
Constitution was promulgated in 1956. He served as
Governor-General for approximately 6 months
before assuming the presidency.
First President of Pakistan: Mirza became the first
president of Pakistan and served from March 23,
1956, to October 27, 1958.
•
Other Political Roles:
o Defence Secretary (1947)
o Governor of East Bengal (1954)
o Interior Minister (1955)
o. Governor-General of Pakistan (1955-1956)
o First President of Pakistan (1956-1958)
Presidency and Ayub Khan's Takeover
•
•
•
During his presidency, Mirza oversaw the
implementation of Pakistan's first constitution and
played a crucial role in shaping the country's early
years.
His presidency was cut short when Ayub Khan, who
was both the Defence Minister and Commander-in-
Chief of the Pakistan Army, ousted him on October
27, 1958. This powerful combination of roles allowed
Ayub Khan to seize control of the government and
establish a military dictatorship in Pakistan.
Despite his controversial legacy, Mirza's political
decisions had a lasting impact on Pakistan's
development. His family's business Interests and
influence continue to be felt in the region.
Ayub Khan's takeover marked a significant turning
point in Pakistani history, leading to a military
dictatorship that lasted for over a decade. It Is
essential to acknowledge the complexities of Ayub
Khan's role in Pakistani politics and the motivations
behind his actions to gain a deeper understanding of
the context and implications of this event.
Exile and Later Life (1958-1969)
Ousted by Ayub Khan in 1958, forced to resign and
leave Pakistan.
Spent his last years in exile in London, England.
Lived a quiet life, away from politics and ublic eye.
Passed away on November 13, 1969, in London, at
the age of 70.
Burial (1969)
Denied burial in Pakistan by the government.
• Buried in Tehran, Iran, at the Imamzadeh Abdullah
Shrine.

Geography
29. Suez Canal links Mediterranean Sea directly to
the: (Red Sea)
Associated Information:
The Suez Canalis a man-made waterway connecting the
Mediterranean Sea to the Red Sea, running through
Egypt. It's a critical shipping lane, allowing vessels to
travel between Europe and Asia without
circumnavigating Africa.
Some key facts about the Suez Canal:
- Constructed between 1859 and 1869 by the Suez Canal
Company
The Suez Canal Company was a French-led consortium
founded in 1858 to construct and operate the canal
The company was granted a 99-year concession to
operate the canal, which expired in 1968
The company's initial capital was 200 million francs,
subscribed by Investors from France, Egypt, and other
countries
Construction took 10 years to complete (1859-1869)
Original construction cost was around $100 million
(approximately $1.5 billion in today's dollars)
Officially opened on November 17, 1869
Length: approximately 120 miles (193 km)
The canal is approximately 120 miles (193 km) long and
700 feet (213 meters) wide
Connects the Mediterranean Sea to the Red Sea
- Allows ships to travel between Europe and Asia without
circumnavigating Africa
Saves time, fuel, and resources for global shipping
One of the most important waterways in the world
Managed and operated by the Suez Canal Authority
(SCA), an Egyptian government agency

--- Page 28 ---
40 Advanced PPSC MCQs Papers (Volume 2)
Undergone several expansions and modernizations to
accommodate larger vessels
It takes an average of 12-16 hours for a ship to pass
through the canal
The canal has no locks, and the water level is
maintained through a system of dams and canals
Nationalized by the Egyptian government In 1956,
leading to the Suez Crisis
The nationalization led to a significant Increase in
Egyptian control over the canal and its revenues
- The Suez Crisis was a major International conflict that
followed, involving Egypt, Britain, France, and Israel
In 2015, the canal underwent a major expansion,
dubbed the "New Suez Canal," which added a new 35-
mile (56 km) parallel channel to allow for two-way traffic
The expansion increased the canal's capacity and
reduced waiting times for ships
- The Suez Canal is a critical route for global trade, with
over 17,000 vessels passing through it in 2020 alone
- It generates significant revenue for Egypt, with tolls
paid by ships using the canal
- The canal has played a significant role in international
politics and conflict, including the Suez Crisis in 1956 and
the Arab-Israell Wars

Everyday Science
30. Why is the Ozone important for mankind?
(Protection from ultraviolet rays of sun)
Associated Information:
Some key points about ozone and the ozone layer:
Ozone (03):
- Ozone is a molecule composed of three oxygen atoms
It is a powerful oxidizing agent and has a pungent,
pleasant smell
Ozone is created naturally in the stratosphere through
the interaction of ultraviolet radiation and oxygen
molecules
It can also be created artificially through electrical
discharges or chemical reactions
Ozone Layer:
The ozone layer is a region in the stratosphere, about
15-30 km above the Earth's surface
- It contains a high concentration of ozone (O3), which
absorbs most of the sun's ultraviolet (UV) radiation
The ozone layer protects living organisms from UV
damage, which can cause skin cancer, cataracts, and
other health issues
- The ozone layer also helps regulate the Earth's climate
and weather patterns
Ozone Depletion:
Chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs) and other halogenated
gases are the main culprits behind ozone depletion
These gases were widely used as refrigerants,
propellants, and solvents in various products
When released into the atmosphere, they can rise to
the stratosphere and catalytically destroy ozone
molecules
The resulting ozone hole over Antarctica was first
discovered in 1985 and has since become an annual
occurrence
Consequences of Ozone Depletion:
Increased UV radiation reaching the Earth's surface.
leading to health issues and environmental damage
Damage to crops and marine ecosystems
Disruption of the Earth's climate and weather patterns
International Efforts to Protect the Ozone Layer:
The Montreal Protocol, signed in 1987, aims to phase
out CFCs and other ozone-depleting substances

61. Decimal form of 5.3% is: (0.053)

71. The ratio of cost price and selling price is 4: 5.
The profit percent is: (25 per cent)
Solution:
Let's break it down:
Ratio of cost price and selling price = 4:5
Let the cost price be 4x and the selling price be 5x.
Profit = Selling price - Cost price = 5x - 4x = x
Profit percentage = (Profit / Cost price) x 100 = (x/4x) x
100 = 25%
So, the profit percent is 25%.

77. A number is as much greater than 36 as is less
than 86. Find the number? (61)
Solution:
Let the number be x. The problem states that the
number is as much greater than 36 as it is less than 86.
This can be expressed as:
x-36-86-xx- 36 = 86-xx-36=86-x
Simplify the equation:
2x=1222x = 1222x=122 x=61x = 61x=61
Thus, the number is 61.

multiply by 3.6.

So, the correct answer is: (B) 50.4 km/h

7. Albert Einstein was born in: (Germany)
Associated Information:
Albert Einstein (1879-1955) was a renowned German.
born physicist who revolutionized our understanding of
the universe. Here are some key aspects of his life and
work:
Theories:
1. Relativity: Einstein's most famous contribution, which
includes:
Special Relativity (1905): Introduced the concept of
time dilation and length contraction.
General Relativity (1915): Described gravity as the
curvature of spacetime.
2. Photons and Quantum Mechanics: Einstein's work an
the photoelectric effect led to the development of
quantum theory.
3. Brownian Motion: Provided mathematical proof of the
existence of atoms and molecules.
4. Equivalence of Energy and Mass: Famous equation:
E=mc²
Awards and Recognition:
1. Nobel Prize in Physics (1921)
2. Copley Medal (1925)
3. Max Planck Medal (1929)
4. Time Magazine's Person of the Century (1999)
Personal Life:
1. Born in Munich, Germany, to a Jewish family
2. Grew up in Switzerland and Germany
3. Married Mileva Marić and had two sons
4. Emigrated to the United States in the 1930s
5. Passionate advocate for peace, civil rights, and nuclear
disarmament

16. The Diamir-Bhasha Dam will be constructed on:
(Indus)
Associated Information:
1. Headwork: A headwork is a small dam or barrier
constructed across a river of channel to divert water,
control flooding, or raise the water level. It's often used
to supply water for irrigation, Industrial, or municipal
purposes.
2. Barrage: A barrage is a type of headwork that spans a
river or estuary to regulate the flow of water, prevent
flooding, or divert water for Irrigation. Unlike a dam, a
barrage doesn't have a reservoir; instead, it allows water
to flow continuously.
3. Dam: A dam is a barrier constructed across a river or
stream to impound water, creating a reservoir. Dams
serve various purposes, such as hydroelectric power
generation, water supply, Irrigation, flood control, and
recreation. Dams are typically larger and more complex
than headworks of barrages.
4. Reservoir: A reservoir is an artificial or natural body of
water created by the construction of a dam or barrier.
Reservoirs store water for various uses, including water
supply, irrigation, hydroelectric power generation, flood
control, and recreation.
5. Lake: A lake is a natural or artificial body of water
surrounded by land. Lakes can be formed by various
factors, including tectonic activity, glaciation, or the
construction of a dam. Lakes serve as habitats for aquatic
ecosystems, support recreational activities, and can be
used for water supply, irrigation, or hydroelectric power
generation.
Key differences:
Purpose: Headwork (diversion, flood control), Barrage
(regulation, diversion), Dam (impoundment, power
generation), Reservoir (water storage), Lake (natural or
artificial water body)
Size and complexity: Headwork (small), Barrage
(medium), Dam (large), Reservoir (varies), Lake (varies)
- Water flow: Headwork (diverts water), Barrage
(regulates flow), Dam (impounds water), Reservoir
(stores water), Lake (contains water)
- Water body: Headwork (no reservoir), Barrage (no
reservoir), Dam (has a reservoir), Reservoir (is a water
storage body), Lake (is a natural or artificial water body)

24. Bio-fuels are sources of energy derived from:
(Plants & Animals)
Associated Information:
Blofuels are fuels produced from organic matter, such as
plants, algae, or agricultural waste. They are a renewable
alternative to fossil fuels and can be used to power
vehicles, heat homes, and generate electricity. Here are
some key points about blofuels:
Types of Blofuels:
1. Bioethanol: Made from fermented plant materials like
corn, sugarcane, or switchgrass.
2. Biodiesel: Produced from vegetable oils or animal fats.
3. Advanced Biofuels: Made from non-food biomass like
agricultural waste, algae, or municipal waste.
4. Synthetic Biofuels: Produced through chemical
reactions using renewable energy.

88. The average of four consecutive odd numbers
is 24. Find the largest number: (27)
Solution:
Let the four consecutive odd numbers be x, x + 2, x + 4,
x + 6.
The average of these four numbers is given as 24:
Simplify the sum in the numerator;
Multiply both sides by 4 to eliminate the denominator:
Subtract 12 from both sides:
Divide by 4:
The four consecutive odd numbers are 21, 23, 25, 27.
The largest number is: (B) 27
89. For a qualification 2/5 of the marks were given
for coursework. The rest of the marks were
given for a written paper of which 3/8 were
given for a mental test. Total marks were out of
120. How many marks were given for the
mental test. (27)
Solution:
Let's break this down step by step:
1. 2/5 of the marks were given for coursework, which
means the coursework portion is:
(2/5) x 120 = 48 marks
1. The rest of the marks were given for the written paper,
which is:
120-4872 marks
1. Of the written paper marks, 3/8 were given for the
mental test, which is:
(3/8) x 72 = 27 marks
So, the correct answer is (C) 27 marks were given for the
mental test.

3. When was China admitted to the UNO? (1971)
Associated Information:
The correct answer is (A) 1971. China was admitted to
the United Nations Organization (UNO) on October 25,
1971, in place of Taiwan, which had previously held the
seat. This followed the passage of Resolution 2758 by the
UN General Assembly, recognizing the People's Republic
of China as the sole legitimate representative of China.
Prior to this, the Republic of China (Taiwan) had been a
founding member of the UN since 1945.

5. Big Ben is in: (London) Or =Sx
Associated Information:
O.C.P
C
Big sen, tower clock, famous for its accuracy and for its
massive bell. Clock's mechanism is known fars
remarkable accuracy and resilience despite its age. Big
Ben's chimes have become a global symbol of London.
Strictly speaking, the name refers to only the great hour
bell, which weighs 15.1 tons (13.7 metric tons), but it is
commonly associated with the whole clock tower at the
northern end of the Houses of Parliament, in the London
borough of Westminster. The tower itself was formally
known as St. Stephen's Tower until 2012, when it was
renamed Elizabeth Tower on the occasion of Elizabeth
II's Diamond Jubilee, celebrating 60 years on the British
throne. The hands of the clock are 9 and 14 feet (2.7 and
4.3 metres) long) (respectively, and the clock tower rises
about 320 feet (97.5 metres). Originally in coordination
with the Royal Greenwich. Observatory, the chimes of Big
Ben, have been broadcast with a few interruptionss
since 1924 (as) as daily time signal obyl the British
Broadcasting Corporation (BBC). The clock was designed
by Sir Edmund Beckett Denison/i Denison in association with Sr
George Airy (then astronomer royal) and the clockmaker

21. When Mohammed Ali Bogra presented Bogra
Formula in the assembly? (October 1953)
Associated Information:
The Bogra Formula was a political agreement reached in
1954 between the East and West Pakistan. It aimed to
address the disparities in representation and resource
allocation between the two wings of Pakistan.
Key aspects of the Bogra Formula:
1. Parity: Equal representation for both wings in the
federal legislature.
2. Provincial autonomy: Greater autonomy for East
Pakistan in managing its internal affairs.
3. Resource allocation: A more equitable distribution of
resources and investments between the two wings.
The formula was an attempt to address the grievances of
Bengalis in East Pakistan, who felt marginalized by the
central government in the Center. However, the
agreement's implementation faced resistance from
various quarters, and its impact was short-lived.
The Bogra Formula is seen as a significant event in the
history of Pakistan, as it highlighted the growing tensions
between the two wings of Pakistan and contributed to
the eventual separation in 1971.

Minister)

25. What document was firstly drafted to give pace
to constitution making process? (Objective
Resolution)
Associated Information:
The Objective Resolution was the first document drafted
to give pace to the constitution-making process in
Pakistan. It was adopted by the Constituent Assembly on
March 12, 1949, and presented by Prime Minister
Liaquat Ali Khan on March 7, 1949. Out of 75 members
of the assembly, 21 voted in opposition to it. The
Objective Resolution laid the foundation for the
constitution-making process in Pakistan, and it's
considered a significant step towards the creation of the
country's constitution.
Key Points:
1. Sovereignty of God: The resolution affirmed the
sovereignty of God and the accountability of all citizens
to Him.
2. Democratic Principles: It enshrined democratic
principles, ensuring that the constitution would be based
on the will of the people.
3. Fundamental Rights: The resolution guaranteed
fundamental rights, including equality, justice, and
freedom of speech, religion, and association.
4. Islamic Principles: It emphasized the Importance of
Islamic principles and values in the governance of
Pakistan.
5. National Unity: The resolution stressed the need for
national unity and solidarity among the people of
Pakistan.
Significance:
1. Foundation for the Constitution: The Objective
Resolution served as a foundation for the constitution
making process, providing a framework for the future
constitution.
2. Guiding Principles: It established guiding principles for
the governance of Pakistan, ensuring that the
constitution would reflect democratic, Islamic, and
human rights values.

31. Which of the following is not a Scandinavian

Associated Information:
The Scandinavian countries are a group of countries
located in Northern Europe, characterized by their
shared cultural, historical, and linguistic heritage. The
term "Scandinavia" typically refers to these 3:
1. Denmark, 2. Norway & 3. Sweden
Sometimes, the term "Nordic countries" is used to
include:
1. Finland, 2. Iceland, 3. Faroe Islands (a Danish territory)
4. Greenland (a Danish territory), 5. Åland Islands (a
Finnish territory)
These countries share similarities in:
1. Language: North Germanic languages (Danish,
Norwegian, Swedish, Icelandic, and Faroese)
2. History: Viking Age, Middle Ages, and modern-day
social democracy
3. Culture: Folk culture, design, literature, and cuisine.
4. Geography: Fjords, mountains, forests, and coastlines
along the Baltic Sea and Atlantic Ocean
The Scandinavian countries are known for their:
1. High standard of living, 2. Social welfare systems, 3.
Education, 4. Innovation, 5. Natural beauty & 6. Viking
heritage

42. The lifespan of Red Blood Cells is __________
days? (120)
Associated Information:
Key Facts About Red Blood Cells (RBCs):
1. Structure: RBCs are biconcave, disk-shaped cells
with a high surface area-to-volume ratio, enabling
efficient gas exchange and passage through narrow
capillaries.
2. Function: RBCs transport oxygen from the lungs to
tissues and carry carbon dioxide back to the lungs,
supporting cellular respiration and homeostasis.
3. Production: Produced in the bone marrow through
erythropoiesis, RBCs have a lifespan of approximately
120 days, after which they are broken down in the
spleen and liver.
4. Components: RBCs contain hemoglobin, a protein
that binds oxygen and gives blood its red color.
Hemoglobin facilitates efficient oxygen and carbon
dioxide transport.
5. Count:
Ο Men: 4.7-6.1 million cells per microliter (mcL).
Ο Women: 4.2-5.4 million cells/mcL.
Ο Children: 4.1-5.5 million cells/mcL.
6. Related Diseases:
Ο Anemia: Low RBC count or hemoglobin, leading to
fatigue and weakness.
Ο Polycythemia: High RBC count, causing thickened
blood and complications.
Ο Sickle Cell Disease: Genetic disorder impairing
oxygen transport due to crescent-shaped RBCs.
Ο Thalassemia: Genetic condition affecting
hemoglobin production.
Key Facts About White Blood Cells (WBCs):
1. Structure and Types: WBCs are larger than RBCs
and exist in various types, including neutrophils,
lymphocytes, monocytes, eosinophils, and
basophils, each with specialized immune functions.
2. Function: WBCs defend the body against infections
and foreign invaders. They identify pathogens,
produce antibodies, and remove dead or damaged
cells, ensuring immune response.
3. Production and Lifespan: WBCs are produced in the
bone marrow and lymphatic tissues. Their lifespan
varies: some, like neutrophils, live a few hours,
while memory lymphocytes may persist for years.
4. Count: Normal WBC count in adults is 4,000-11,000
cells per microliter (mcL) of blood.
5. Related Diseases:
Ο Leukopenia: Low WBC count, increasing
susceptibility to infections.
Ο Leukocytosis: High WBC count, often indicating
infection or inflammation.
Ο Leukemia: Cancer of blood-forming tissues, leading
to abnormal WBC production.
Ο Lymphoma: Cancer affecting lymphocytes.
Graveyards of Red and White Blood Cells:
1. Graveyard of RBCs:
Ο Spleen: Known as the "graveyard of RBCs," the
spleen filters and breaks down old or damaged
cells.
▪ Heme: Converted to bilirubin, excreted in bile.
▪ Iron: Recycled for new RBC production.
▪ Globin: Broken into amino acids for reuse.
Ο Liver: Plays a secondary role in processing RBC
components and excreting bilirubin.
2. Graveyard of WBCs:
Ο Tissues: Most WBCs die at infection or
inflammation sites after fulfilling their immune role.
Ο Spleen and Liver: Remove non-functional or dead
WBCs from circulation.
Ο Bone Marrow: Some WBCs are recycled and
replaced in the bone marrow.
Conclusion:
RBCs ensure oxygen transport and carbon dioxide
removal, vital for cellular function, while WBCs protect
the body from infections and maintain immunity. Their
unique production, functionality, and breakdown
processes underscore their significance in sustaining life
and health.

g/cm³)

48. Who is the father of Computer? (Charles
Babbage)
Associated Information:
Key Personalities in the Computer World
1. Charles Babbage (1791-1871) - Father of the
Computer (Designed the Analytical Engine, the first
mechanical computer concept.)
2. Alan Turing (1912-1954) - Father of Computer
Science
and
Artificial
Intelligence
(Pioneered theoretical computer science and AI;
developed the Turing Machine.)
3. John Atanasoff (1903-1995) - Father of the
Electronic Computer (Developed the Atanasoff-
Berry Computer (ABC), the first electronic digital
computer.)
4. John Mauchly (1907-1980) - Co-Father of the
General-Purpose Electronic Computer (ENIAC)
(Collaborated with Eckert on ENIAC, the first
general-purpose electronic digital computer.)
5. J. Presper Eckert (1919-1995) - Co-Father of the
General-Purpose Electronic Computer (ENIAC)

--- Page 2 ---
Advanced PPSC MCQs Papers (Volume 2)
(Worked with Mauchly on ENIAC, a general-purpose
computing.)
6. Steve Jobs (1955-2011) - Visionary of the Personal
Computer Revolution (Co-founder of Apple; key
figure in the development of Apple I, Apple II, and
Macintosh.)
7. Steve Wozniak (1950-present) - Father of the
Personal Computer (Co-founder of Apple; designed
the hardware and software for the Apple I and
Apple II.)
8. Don Estridge (1939-1985) - Father of the IBM PC

50. Who is the father of Internet? (Vint Cerf)
Associated Information:
The "Father of the Internet" is a title often bestowed
upon Vint Cerf and Bob Kahn, two American computer
scientists who played a pivotal role in the development
of the Internet as we know it today.
Vint Cerf and Bob Kahn designed the basic architecture
of the Internet, including:
1. TCP/IP (Transmission Control Protocol/Internet
Protocol): The fundamental communication protocols
that enable data exchange over the Internet.
2. IP addresses: The system of unique addresses that
identify devices on the Internet.
3. Packet switching: The method of breaking data into
small packets for efficient transmission over networks.
Their work in the 1970s and 1980s, funded by the US
Department of Defense's Advanced Research Projects
Agency (ARPA), laid the foundation for the modern
Internet. Cerf and Kahn's contributions have had a
profound impact on the development of the Internet,
enabling global communication, information sharing,
and e-commerce.
Vint Cerf is also known for his continued advocacy for
Internet accessibility, security, and privacy, while Bob
Kahn has focused on the development of new network
technologies and the promotion of Internet access for
all.

58. Complete: 5, 16, 6, 16, 7, 16, __________ (12)
Solution:
Let's carefully analyze the sequence:
Sequence: 5, 16, 6, 16, 7, 16, 9, __________

--- Page 3 ---
Step-by-Step Pattern Analysis:
1. Odd-Position Numbers (Non-16s):
Ο The sequence alternates between a changing
number and the constant 16.
Ο Look at the pattern for odd-position numbers:
▪ 1st: 5
▪ 3rd: 6 (5+1)
▪ 5th: 7 (6+1)
▪ 7th: 9 (7+2)
▪ The increment alternates between +1 and +2.
Next number (9+2): 12
2. Even-Position Numbers:
Ο The even positions are always 16.
Final Sequence:
5, 16, 6, 16, 7, 16, 9, 16, 12, __________

98. What was the name of Imam Bukhari (R.A)?
(Muhammad bin Ismaeel)
Associated Information:
Imam Bukhari (810-870 CE): Pioneered in Islamic
Scholarship
Full Name: Muhammad ibn Isma'il ibn Ibrahim ibn al-
Mughirah ibn Bardizbah al-Bukhari. Imam Bukhari was a
renowned Islamic scholar and theologian, best known
for compiling "Sahih al-Bukhari," one of the "most
authentic and influential collections of Hadith (Prophetic
traditions) in Islamic literature."

--- Page 4 ---
56
Advanced PPSC MCQs Papers (Volume 2)
Contributions of Imam Bukhari:
1. Hadith Collection: Imam Bukhari traveled
extensively across the Islamic world, collecting over
600,000 hadiths. Through meticulous scrutiny, he
compiled Sahih al-Bukhari, which contains
approximately 7,275 narrations (including
repetitions), regarded as one of the most reliable
collections.
2. Authentication Methodology: He pioneered a
rigorous system to verify the authenticity of
hadiths, emphasizing the integrity and reliability of
narrators as well as the isnad (chain of
transmission). His criteria remain a cornerstone of
Islamic hadith science.
3. Islamic Scholarship: His work profoundly impacted
Islamic theology, jurisprudence, and history,
providing a solid foundation for understanding the
life and teachings of Prophet Muhammad (PBUH).
4. Teaching and Mentorship: Imam Bukhari mentored
numerous students and scholars, spreading
knowledge and fostering Islamic scholarship in his
era and beyond.
Impact: Imam Bukhari's dedication to authenticating
hadiths and his profound scholarship have made him a
central figure in Islamic intellectual history. His work
continues to guide and inspire Muslims worldwide,
underscoring his enduring legacy in the study of Islam.
